      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Menelaion.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1579" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Menelaion.jpg/280px-Menelaion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Menelaion.jpg/420px-Menelaion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Menelaion.jpg/560px-Menelaion.jpg 2x" width="280"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Menelaion.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Hollow Lacedaemon. Site of the Menelaion, the ancient shrine to Helen and Menelaus constructed in the Bronze Age city that stood on the hill of
         <a href="/wiki/Therapnes" title="Therapnes">
          Therapne
         </a>
         on the left bank of the
         <a href="/wiki/Eurotas_(river)" title="Eurotas (river)">
          Eurotas River
         </a>
         overlooking the future site of Dorian Sparta. Across the valley the successive ridges of Mount
         <a href="/wiki/Taygetus" title="Taygetus">
          Taygetus
         </a>
         are in evidence.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <b>
        Sparta
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Doric_Greek" title="Doric Greek">
        Doric Greek
       </a>
       : Σπάρτα,
       <i>
        Spártā
       </i>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">
        Attic Greek
       </a>
       :
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%A3%CF%80%CE%AC%CF%81%CF%84%CE%B7" title="wikt:Σπάρτη">
        Σπάρτη
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Spártē
       </i>
       ) was a prominent
       <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">
        city-state
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
        ancient Greece
       </a>
       . In antiquity the city-state was known as
       <b>
        Lacedaemon
       </b>
       (
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%9B%CE%B1%CE%BA%CE%B5%CE%B4%CE%B1%CE%AF%CE%BC%CF%89%CE%BD" title="wikt:Λακεδαίμων">
         Λακεδαίμων
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         Lakedaímōn
        </i>
       </span>
       ), while the name Sparta referred to its main settlement on the banks of the
       <a href="/wiki/Eurotas_(river)" title="Eurotas (river)">
        Eurotas River
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Laconia" title="Laconia">
        Laconia
       </a>
       , in south-eastern
       <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">
        Peloponnese
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Around 650 BC, it rose to become the dominant military land-power in ancient Greece.
      </p>
      <p>
       Given its military pre-eminence, Sparta was recognized as the overall leader of the combined Greek forces during the
       <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">
        Greco-Persian Wars
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Between 431 and 404 BC, Sparta was the principal enemy of
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">
        Athens
       </a>
       during the
       <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">
        Peloponnesian War
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       from which it emerged victorious, though at a great cost of lives lost. Sparta's defeat by
       <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">
        Thebes
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leuctra" title="Battle of Leuctra">
        Battle of Leuctra
       </a>
       in 371 BC ended Sparta's prominent role in Greece. However, it maintained its political independence until the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Greece" title="Roman Greece">
        Roman conquest of Greece
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Corinth_(146_BC)" title="Battle of Corinth (146 BC)">
        146 BC
       </a>
       . It then underwent a long period of decline, especially in the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
        Middle Ages
       </a>
       , when many Spartans moved to live in
       <a href="/wiki/Mystras" title="Mystras">
        Mystras
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sparti" title="Sparti">
        Modern Sparta
       </a>
       is the capital of the Greek regional unit of
       <a href="/wiki/Laconia" title="Laconia">
        Laconia
       </a>
       and a center for the processing of goods such as citrus and olives.
      </p>
      <p>
       Sparta was unique in
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
        ancient Greece
       </a>
       for its social system and
       <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">
        constitution
       </a>
       , which configured their entire society to maximize military proficiency at all costs, and completely focused on military training and excellence. Its inhabitants were classified as
       <a href="/wiki/Spartiate" title="Spartiate">
        Spartiates
       </a>
       (Spartan citizens, who enjoyed full rights),
       <a href="/wiki/Mothax" title="Mothax">
        mothakes
       </a>
       (non-Spartan free men raised as Spartans),
       <a href="/wiki/Perioeci" title="Perioeci">
        perioikoi
       </a>
       (free residents, literally "dwellers around"), and
       <a href="/wiki/Helots" title="Helots">
        helots
       </a>
       (state-owned serfs, enslaved non-Spartan local population). Spartiates underwent the rigorous
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Agoge" title="Agoge">
         agoge
        </a>
       </i>
       training and education regimen, and Spartan
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalanx_formation" title="Phalanx formation">
        phalanges
       </a>
       were widely considered to be among the best in battle. Spartan women enjoyed considerably more rights and equality to men than elsewhere in the
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">
        classical antiquity
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Sparta was the subject of fascination in its own day, as well as in
       <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">
        Western culture
       </a>
       following the revival of classical learning.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [n 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This love or admiration of Sparta is known as
       <a href="/wiki/Laconophilia" title="Laconophilia">
        Laconism or Laconophilia
       </a>
       . At its peak around 500 BC the size of the city would have been some 20,000–35,000 citizens, plus numerous helots and perioikoi. The likely total of 40,000–50,000 made Sparta one of the largest Greek cities;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       however, according to Thucydides, the population of Athens in 431 BC was 360,000–610,000, making it unlikely that Athens was smaller than Sparta in 5th century BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [n 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The French classicist François Ollier in his 1933 book
       <i>
        Le mirage spartiate
       </i>
       ("
       <i>
        The Spartan Mirage
       </i>
       ") warned that a major scholarly problem regarding Sparta is that all the surviving accounts were written by non-Spartans who often presented an excessively idealized image of Sparta.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodkinson_pages_222-281_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hodkinson_pages_222-281-9">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ollier's views have been widely accepted by scholars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodkinson_pages_222-281_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hodkinson_pages_222-281-9">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Names">
        Names
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The earliest attested term referring to Lacedaemon is the
       <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">
        Mycenaean Greek
       </a>
       <span lang="gmy" xml:lang="gmy">
        𐀨𐀐𐀅𐀖𐀛𐀍
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        ra-ke-da-mi-ni-jo
       </i>
       , "Lacedaimonian", written in
       <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">
        Linear B
       </a>
       syllabic script,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaeolexicon-rakedaminijo_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Palaeolexicon-rakedaminijo-10">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [n 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       being the equivalent of the written in the Greek alphabet, latter Greek,
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        Λακεδαιμόνιος
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        Lakedaimonios
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Lacedaemonius
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L&amp;S-Lacedaemon_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-L&amp;S-Lacedaemon-18">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eurotas.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Eurotas.JPG/280px-Eurotas.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Eurotas.JPG/420px-Eurotas.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Eurotas.JPG/560px-Eurotas.JPG 2x" width="280"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eurotas.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Eurotas River
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The ancient Greeks used one of three words to refer to the home location of the Spartans. The first refers primarily to the main cluster of settlements in the valley of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurotas_River" title="Eurotas River">
        Eurotas River
       </a>
       : Sparta.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The second word was Lacedaemon (
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        Λακεδαίμων
       </span>
       );
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       this was also used sometimes as an adjective and is the name commonly used in the works of
       <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">
        Homer
       </a>
       and the historians
       <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
        Herodotus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">
        Thucydides
       </a>
       . Herodotus seems to denote by it the
       <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">
        Mycenaean Greek
       </a>
       citadel at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therapne" title="Therapne">
        Therapne
       </a>
       , in contrast to the lower town of Sparta. It could be used synonymously with Sparta, but typically it was not. It denoted the terrain on which Sparta was situated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Homer it is typically combined with epithets of the countryside: wide, lovely, shining and most often hollow and broken (full of ravines).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The hollow suggests the Eurotas Valley. Sparta on the other hand is the country of lovely women, a people epithet.
      </p>
      <p>
       The name of the population was often used for the state of Lacedaemon: the Lacedaemonians. This epithet utilized the plural of the adjective Lacedaemonius (Greek:
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        Λακεδαιμὀνιοι
       </span>
       ; Latin:
       <i>
        Lacedaemonii
       </i>
       , but also
       <i>
        Lacedaemones
       </i>
       ). If the ancients wished to refer to the country more directly, instead of Lacedaemon, they could use a back-formation from the adjective:
       <i>
        Lacedaemonian country
       </i>
       . As most words for "country" were feminine, the adjective was in the feminine:
       <i>
        Lacedaemonia
       </i>
       (
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        Λακεδαιμονία
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        Lakedaimonia
       </i>
       ). Eventually, the adjective came to be used alone.
      </p>
      <p>
       "Lacedaemonia" was not in general use during the classical period and before. It does occur in Greek as an equivalent of Laconia and Messenia during the Roman and early Byzantine periods, mostly in ethnographers and lexica glossing place names. For example,
       <a href="/wiki/Hesychius_of_Alexandria" title="Hesychius of Alexandria">
        Hesychius of Alexandria
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        Lexicon
       </i>
       (5th century AD) defines Agiadae as a "place in Lacedaemonia" named after Agis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The actual transition may be captured by
       <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">
        Isidore of Seville
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        Etymologiae
       </i>
       (7th century AD), an etymological dictionary. He relied heavily on
       <a href="/wiki/Orosius" title="Orosius">
        Orosius
       </a>
       '
       <i>
        Historiarum Adversum Paganos
       </i>
       (5th century AD) and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">
        Eusebius of Caesarea
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chronicon_(Eusebius)" title="Chronicon (Eusebius)">
         Chronicon
        </a>
       </i>
       (early 5th century AD) as did Orosius. The latter defines Sparta to be
       <i>
        Lacedaemonia Civitas
       </i>
       but Isidore defines Lacedaemonia as founded by Lacedaemon, son of Semele, relying on Eusebius.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is a rare use, perhaps the earliest of Lacedaemonia, in
       <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">
        Diodorus Siculus
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but probably with
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        Χὠρα
       </span>
       ("country") suppressed.
      </p>
      <p>
       The immediate area around the town of Sparta, the plateau east of the Taygetos mountains, was generally referred as Laconice (
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        Λακωνική
       </span>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This term was sometimes used to refer to all the regions under direct Spartan control, including
       <a href="/wiki/Messenia_(ancient_region)" title="Messenia (ancient region)">
        Messenia
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Lakedaimona was until 2006 the name of a
       <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Greece" title="Provinces of Greece">
        province
       </a>
       in the modern Greek
       <a href="/wiki/Prefectures_of_Greece" title="Prefectures of Greece">
        prefecture
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Laconia" title="Laconia">
        Laconia
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Sparta is located in the region of Laconia, in the south-eastern Peloponnese. Ancient Sparta was built on the banks of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurotas_River" title="Eurotas River">
        Eurotas River
       </a>
       , the main river of Laconia, which provided it with a source of fresh water. The valley of the Eurotas is a natural fortress, bounded to the west by
       <a href="/wiki/Taygetus" title="Taygetus">
        Mt. Taygetus
       </a>
       (2407 m) and to the east by
       <a href="/wiki/Parnon" title="Parnon">
        Mt. Parnon
       </a>
       (1935 m). To the north, Laconia is separated from
       <a href="/wiki/Arcadia" title="Arcadia">
        Arcadia
       </a>
       by hilly uplands reaching 1000 m in altitude. These natural defenses worked to Sparta's advantage and contributed to Sparta never having been sacked. Though landlocked, Sparta had a harbor,
       <a href="/wiki/Gytheio" title="Gytheio">
        Gytheio
       </a>
       , on the Laconian Gulf.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">
        Mythology
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Lacedaemon_(mythology)" title="Lacedaemon (mythology)">
        Lacedaemon
       </a>
       (Greek:
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        Λακεδαίμων
       </span>
       ) was a
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">
        mythical
       </a>
       king of Laconia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PausaniasIII.1.2_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PausaniasIII.1.2-27">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The son of
       <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">
        Zeus
       </a>
       by the nymph
       <a href="/wiki/Taygete" title="Taygete">
        Taygete
       </a>
       , he married
       <a href="/wiki/Sparta_(mythology)" title="Sparta (mythology)">
        Sparta
       </a>
       , the daughter of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurotas_(mythology)" title="Eurotas (mythology)">
        Eurotas
       </a>
       , by whom he became the father of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Amyclas_of_Sparta" title="King Amyclas of Sparta">
        Amyclas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Eurydice_of_Argos" title="Eurydice of Argos">
        Eurydice
       </a>
       , and Asine. He named the country after himself and the city after his wife.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PausaniasIII.1.2_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-PausaniasIII.1.2-27">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He was believed to have built the sanctuary of the
       <a href="/wiki/Charites" title="Charites">
        Charites
       </a>
       , which stood between Sparta and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amyclae" title="Amyclae">
        Amyclae
       </a>
       , and to have given to those divinities the names of
       <a href="/wiki/Cleta" title="Cleta">
        Cleta
       </a>
       and Phaenna. A
       <a href="/wiki/Heroon" title="Heroon">
        shrine
       </a>
       was erected to him in the neighborhood of Therapne.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology_of_the_classical_period">
        Archaeology of the classical period
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_sparta_theater.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="786" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ancient_sparta_theater.jpg/220px-Ancient_sparta_theater.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ancient_sparta_theater.jpg/330px-Ancient_sparta_theater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ancient_sparta_theater.jpg/440px-Ancient_sparta_theater.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_sparta_theater.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The theater of ancient Sparta with
         <a href="/wiki/Taygetus" title="Taygetus">
          Mt. Taygetus
         </a>
         in the background.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">
        Thucydides
       </a>
       wrote:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        Suppose the city of Sparta to be deserted, and nothing left but the temples and the ground-plan, distant ages would be very unwilling to believe that the power of the Lacedaemonians was at all equal to their fame. Their city is not built continuously, and has no splendid temples or other edifices; it rather resembles a group of villages, like the ancient towns of Hellas, and would therefore make a poor show.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
         <a href="#cite_note-28">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Until the early 20th century, the chief ancient buildings at Sparta were the theatre, of which, however, little showed above ground except portions of the
       <a href="/wiki/Retaining_wall" title="Retaining wall">
        retaining walls
       </a>
       ; the so-called
       <i>
        Tomb of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leonidas" title="Leonidas">
         Leonidas
        </a>
       </i>
       , a quadrangular building, perhaps a temple, constructed of immense blocks of stone and containing two chambers; the foundation of an ancient bridge over the
       <a href="/wiki/Eurotas" title="Eurotas">
        Eurotas
       </a>
       ; the ruins of a circular structure; some remains of late Roman fortifications; several brick buildings and mosaic pavements.
      </p>
      <p>
       The remaining archaeological wealth consisted of inscriptions, sculptures, and other objects collected in the local museum, founded by Stamatakis in 1872 and enlarged in 1907. Partial excavation of the round building was undertaken in 1892 and 1893 by the
       <a href="/wiki/American_School_of_Classical_Studies_at_Athens" title="American School of Classical Studies at Athens">
        American School at Athens
       </a>
       . The structure has been since found to be a semicircular retaining wall of Hellenic origin that was partly restored during the Roman period.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sparta_ruins.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="372" data-file-width="258" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Sparta_ruins.PNG/220px-Sparta_ruins.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/Sparta_ruins.PNG 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sparta_ruins.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Ruins" title="Ruins">
          Ruins
         </a>
         from the ancient site
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1904, the
       <a href="/wiki/British_School_at_Athens" title="British School at Athens">
        British School at Athens
       </a>
       began a thorough exploration of
       <a href="/wiki/Laconia" title="Laconia">
        Laconia
       </a>
       , and in the following year excavations were made at Thalamae, Geronthrae, and Angelona near
       <a href="/wiki/Monemvasia" title="Monemvasia">
        Monemvasia
       </a>
       . In 1906, excavations began in Sparta.
      </p>
      <p>
       A small circus described by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Martin_Leake" title="William Martin Leake">
        Leake
       </a>
       proved to be a theatre-like building constructed soon after AD 200 around the altar and in front of the temple of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemis_Orthia" title="Artemis Orthia">
        Artemis Orthia
       </a>
       . Here musical and gymnastic contests took place as well as the famous flogging ordeal (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sanctuary_of_Artemis_Orthia#Winged_Artemis#Diamastigosis" title="Sanctuary of Artemis Orthia">
         diamastigosis
        </a>
       </i>
       ). The temple, which can be dated to the 2nd century BC, rests on the foundation of an older temple of the 6th century, and close beside it were found the remains of a yet earlier temple, dating from the 9th or even the 10th century. The
       <a href="/wiki/Votive_offering" title="Votive offering">
        votive offerings
       </a>
       in clay, amber, bronze, ivory and lead found in great profusion within the precinct range, dating from the 9th to the 4th centuries BC, supply invaluable evidence for early Spartan art.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1907, the sanctuary of Athena "of the Brazen House" (
       <i>
        Chalkioikos
       </i>
       ) was located on the acropolis immediately above the theatre, and though the actual temple is almost completely destroyed, the site has produced the longest extant archaic inscription of Laconia, numerous bronze nails and plates, and a considerable number of votive offerings. The Greek
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City-wall" title="City-wall">
        city-wall
       </a>
       , built in successive stages from the 4th to the 2nd century, was traced for a great part of its circuit, which measured 48 stades or nearly 10 km (6 miles) (Polyb. 1X. 21). The late Roman wall enclosing the acropolis, part of which probably dates from the years following the Gothic raid of AD 262, was also investigated. Besides the actual buildings discovered, a number of points were situated and mapped in a general study of Spartan topography, based upon the description of
       <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">
        Pausanias
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Menelaion">
        Menelaion
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Menelaion,_Sparta" title="Menelaion, Sparta">
        Menelaion, Sparta
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Menelaion is a shrine associated with Menelaus, located east of Sparta, by the river Eurotas, on the hill
       <i>
        Profitis Ilias
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">
        Coordinates
       </a>
       :
       <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
        <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Sparta&amp;params=37.0659_N_22.4536_E_type:landmark_region:GR_scale:500">
         <span class="geo-nondefault">
          <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           <span class="latitude">
            37°03′57″N
           </span>
           <span class="longitude">
            22°27′13″E
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
         <span class="geo-multi-punct">
          ﻿ / ﻿
         </span>
         <span class="geo-default">
          <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           37.0659°N 22.4536°E
          </span>
          <span style="display:none">
           ﻿ /
           <span class="geo">
            37.0659; 22.4536
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       ). Built early 8th century BC it was believed by Spartans to be the home of Menelaus. In 1970 the British School in Athens started excavations in an attempt to locate Mycenaean remains in the area around Menelaion. Among other findings, they uncovered the remains of two Mycenaean mansions and found the first offerings dedicated to Helen and Menelaus. These mansions were destroyed by earthquake and fire, and archaeologists consider them the possible palace of Menelaus himself.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS">
          <span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (November 2017)">
           better source needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Excavations made from the early 1990s to the present suggest that the area around Menelaion in the southern part of the Eurotas valley seems to have been the center of Mycenaean Laconia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Mycenaean settlement was roughly triangular in shape, with its apex pointed towards the north. Its area was approximately equal to that of the "newer" Sparta, but denudation has wreaked havoc with its buildings and nothing is left save ruined foundations and broken potsherds.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sparta" title="History of Sparta">
        History of Sparta
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span id="Prehistory.2C_.22dark_age.22_and_archaic_period">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='Prehistory,_"dark_age"_and_archaic_period'>
        Prehistory, "dark age" and archaic period
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The prehistory of Sparta is difficult to reconstruct because the literary evidence is far removed in time from the events it describes and is also distorted by oral tradition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodot,_Book_I,_56.3_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herodot,_Book_I,_56.3-31">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the earliest certain evidence of human settlement in the region of Sparta consists of
       <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">
        pottery
       </a>
       dating from the Middle
       <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">
        Neolithic
       </a>
       period, found in the vicinity of Kouphovouno some two kilometres (1.2 miles) south-southwest of Sparta.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These are the earliest traces of the original
       <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">
        Mycenaean
       </a>
       Spartan civilisation, as represented in Homer's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">
         Iliad
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This civilization seems to have fallen into decline by the late
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">
        Bronze Age
       </a>
       , when, according to Herodotus, Macedonian tribes from the north (called
       <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">
        Dorians
       </a>
       by those they conquered) marched into Peloponnese and, subjugating the local tribes, settled there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodot,_Book_I,_56.3_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herodot,_Book_I,_56.3-31">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Dorians seem to have set about expanding the frontiers of Spartan territory almost before they had established their own state.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrenberg_2004_31_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ehrenberg_2004_31-33">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They fought against the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argive" title="Argive">
        Argive
       </a>
       Dorians to the east and southeast, and also the
       <a href="/wiki/Arcadia" title="Arcadia">
        Arcadian
       </a>
       Achaeans to the northwest. The evidence suggests that Sparta, relatively inaccessible because of the topography of the Taygetan plain, was secure from early on: it was never fortified.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrenberg_2004_31_33-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ehrenberg_2004_31-33">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lycurgus.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="216" data-file-width="162" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Lycurgus.jpg/150px-Lycurgus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/Lycurgus.jpg 1.5x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lycurgus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Lycurgus_of_Sparta" title="Lycurgus of Sparta">
          Lycurgus
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Nothing distinctive in the archaeology of the Eurotas River Valley identifies the Dorians or the Dorian Spartan state. The prehistory of the Neolithic, the Bronze Age and the Dark Age (the Early Iron Age) at this moment must be treated apart from the stream of Dorian Spartan history.
      </p>
      <p>
       The legendary period of Spartan history is believed to fall into the Dark Age. It treats the mythic heroes such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heraclids" title="Heraclids">
        Heraclids
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Perseides" title="Perseides">
        Perseids
       </a>
       , offering a view of the occupation of the Peloponnesus that contains both fantastic and possibly historical elements. The subsequent proto-historic period, combining both legend and historical fragments, offers the first credible history.
      </p>
      <p>
       Between the 8th and 7th centuries BC the Spartans experienced a period of lawlessness and civil strife, later attested by both Herodotus and Thucydides.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result, they carried out a series of political and social reforms of their own society which they later attributed to a semi-mythical lawgiver,
       <a href="/wiki/Lycurgus_of_Sparta" title="Lycurgus of Sparta">
        Lycurgus
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These reforms mark the beginning of the history of Classical Sparta.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Sparta">
        Classical Sparta
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Messenian_War" title="Second Messenian War">
        Second Messenian War
       </a>
       , Sparta established itself as a local power in Peloponnesus and the rest of Greece. During the following centuries, Sparta's reputation as a land-fighting force was unequalled.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 480 BC a small force of Spartans, Thespians, and Thebans led by King
       <a href="/wiki/Leonidas_I" title="Leonidas I">
        Leonidas
       </a>
       (approximately 300 were full Spartiates, 700 were Thespians, and 400 were Thebans although these numbers do not reflect casualties incurred prior to the final battle), made a legendary
       <a href="/wiki/Last_stand" title="Last stand">
        last stand
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">
        Battle of Thermopylae
       </a>
       against the massive Persian army, inflicting very high casualties on the Persian forces before finally being encircled.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The superior weaponry, strategy, and
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">
        bronze
       </a>
       armour of the Greek
       <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">
        hoplites
       </a>
       and their
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalanx_formation" title="Phalanx formation">
        phalanx
       </a>
       again proved their worth one year later when Sparta assembled at full strength and led a Greek alliance against the Persians at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">
        battle of Plataea
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The decisive Greek victory at Plataea put an end to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_War" title="Greco-Persian War">
        Greco-Persian War
       </a>
       along with Persian ambition of expanding into Europe. Even though this war was won by a pan-Greek army, credit was given to Sparta, who besides being the protagonist at Thermopylae and Plataea, had been the de facto leader of the entire Greek expedition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In later Classical times, Sparta along with
       <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
        Athens
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">
        Thebes
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
        Persia
       </a>
       had been the main powers fighting for supremacy against each other. As a result of the
       <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">
        Peloponnesian War
       </a>
       , Sparta, a traditionally continental culture, became a naval power. At the peak of its power Sparta subdued many of the key Greek states and even managed to overpower the elite Athenian navy. By the end of the 5th century BC it stood out as a state which had defeated the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athenian_Empire" title="Athenian Empire">
        Athenian Empire
       </a>
       and had invaded the Persian provinces in Anatolia, a period which marks the
       <a href="/wiki/Spartan_hegemony" title="Spartan hegemony">
        Spartan Hegemony
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       During the
       <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_War" title="Corinthian War">
        Corinthian War
       </a>
       Sparta faced a coalition of the leading Greek states:
       <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">
        Thebes
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
        Athens
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">
        Corinth
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Argos" title="Argos">
        Argos
       </a>
       . The alliance was initially backed by Persia, whose lands in
       <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">
        Anatolia
       </a>
       had been invaded by Sparta and which feared further Spartan expansion into Asia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sparta achieved a series of land victories, but many of her ships were destroyed at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cnidus" title="Battle of Cnidus">
        battle of Cnidus
       </a>
       by a Greek-Phoenician mercenary fleet that Persia had provided to Athens. The event severely damaged Sparta's naval power but did not end its aspirations of invading further into Persia, until
       <a href="/wiki/Conon" title="Conon">
        Conon
       </a>
       the Athenian ravaged the Spartan coastline and provoked the old Spartan fear of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helot" title="Helot">
        helot
       </a>
       revolt.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boardman_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-boardman-40">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After a few more years of fighting, in 387 BC the
       <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Antalcidas" title="Peace of Antalcidas">
        Peace of Antalcidas
       </a>
       was established, according to which all Greek cities of
       <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">
        Ionia
       </a>
       would return to Persian control, and Persia's Asian border would be free of the Spartan threat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boardman_40-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-boardman-40">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The effects of the war were to reaffirm Persia's ability to interfere successfully in Greek politics and to affirm Sparta's weakened hegemonic position in the Greek political system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sparta entered its long-term decline after a severe military defeat to
       <a href="/wiki/Epaminondas" title="Epaminondas">
        Epaminondas
       </a>
       of Thebes at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leuctra" title="Battle of Leuctra">
        Battle of Leuctra
       </a>
       . This was the first time that a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spartan_Army" title="Spartan Army">
        Spartan army
       </a>
       lost a land battle at full strength.
      </p>
      <p>
       As Spartan citizenship was inherited by blood, Sparta now increasingly faced a helot population that vastly outnumbered its citizens. The alarming decline of Spartan citizens was commented on by
       <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
        Aristotle
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_and_Roman_Sparta">
        Hellenistic and Roman Sparta
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Sparta never fully recovered from the losses that the Spartans suffered at Leuctra in 371 BC and the subsequent
       <a href="/wiki/Helots#Revolts" title="Helots">
        helot revolts
       </a>
       . Nonetheless, it was able to continue as a regional power for over two centuries. Neither
       <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">
        Philip II
       </a>
       nor his son
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">
        Alexander the Great
       </a>
       attempted to conquer Sparta itself.
      </p>
      <p>
       Even during its decline, Sparta never forgot its claim to be the "defender of Hellenism" and its
       <a href="/wiki/Laconic_phrase" title="Laconic phrase">
        Laconic wit
       </a>
       . An anecdote has it that when Philip II sent a message to Sparta saying "If I enter Laconia, I will raze Sparta", the Spartans responded with the single, terse reply:
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        αἴκα
       </span>
       , "if".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When Philip created the
       <a href="/wiki/League_of_Corinth" title="League of Corinth">
        league of the Greeks
       </a>
       on the pretext of unifying Greece against Persia, the Spartans chose not to join, since they had no interest in joining a pan-Greek expedition unless it were under Spartan leadership. Thus, upon the conquest of Persia, Alexander the Great sent to Athens 300 suits of Persian armour with the following inscription:
       <i>
        Alexander, son of Philip, and all the Greeks
        <b>
         except the Spartans
        </b>
        , give these offerings taken from the foreigners who live in Asia
       </i>
       [emphasis added].
      </p>
      <p>
       During Alexander's campaigns in the east, the Spartan king,
       <a href="/wiki/Agis_III" title="Agis III">
        Agis III
       </a>
       sent a force to Crete in 333 BC with the aim of securing the island for Sparta.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Agis next took command of allied Greek forces against Macedon, gaining early successes, before laying siege to
       <a href="/wiki/Megalopolis,_Greece" title="Megalopolis, Greece">
        Megalopolis
       </a>
       in 331 BC. A large Macedonian army under general
       <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">
        Antipater
       </a>
       marched to its relief and defeated the Spartan-led force in a pitched battle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       More than 5,300 of the Spartans and their allies were killed in battle, and 3,500 of Antipater's troops.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Agis, now wounded and unable to stand, ordered his men to leave him behind to face the advancing Macedonian army so that he could buy them time to retreat. On his knees, the Spartan king slew several enemy soldiers before being finally killed by a javelin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Alexander was merciful, and he only forced the Spartans to join the League of Corinth, which they had previously refused to join.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the
       <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">
        Punic Wars
       </a>
       Sparta was an ally of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">
        Roman Republic
       </a>
       . Spartan political independence was put to an end when it was eventually forced into the
       <a href="/wiki/Achaean_League" title="Achaean League">
        Achaean League
       </a>
       after its defeat in the decisive
       <a href="/wiki/War_against_Nabis" title="War against Nabis">
        Laconian War
       </a>
       by a coalition of other Greek city-states and Rome and the resultant overthrow of its final king
       <a href="/wiki/Nabis" title="Nabis">
        Nabis
       </a>
       . Sparta played no active part in the
       <a href="/wiki/Achaean_War" title="Achaean War">
        Achaean War
       </a>
       in 146 BC when the
       <a href="/wiki/Achaean_League" title="Achaean League">
        Achaean League
       </a>
       was defeated by the Roman general
       <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Mummius_Achaicus" title="Lucius Mummius Achaicus">
        Lucius Mummius
       </a>
       . Subsequently, Sparta become a
       <a href="/wiki/Free_city_(classical_antiquity)" title="Free city (classical antiquity)">
        free city
       </a>
       in the Roman sense, some of the institutions of
       <a href="/wiki/Lycurgus_of_Sparta" title="Lycurgus of Sparta">
        Lycurgus
       </a>
       were restored
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the city became a tourist attraction for the Roman elite who came to observe exotic Spartan customs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [n 4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Postclassical_and_modern_Sparta">
        Postclassical and modern Sparta
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       According to Byzantine sources,
       <a href="/wiki/Maniots" title="Maniots">
        some parts
       </a>
       of the Laconian region remained
       <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">
        pagan
       </a>
       until well into the 10th century AD.
       <a href="/wiki/Doric_Greek" title="Doric Greek">
        Doric
       </a>
       -speaking populations survive today in
       <a href="/wiki/Tsakonia" title="Tsakonia">
        Tsakonia
       </a>
       . In the Middle Ages, the political and cultural center of Laconia shifted to the nearby settlement of
       <a href="/wiki/Mystras" title="Mystras">
        Mystras
       </a>
       , and Sparta fell further in even local importance. Modern
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sparti_(municipality)" title="Sparti (municipality)">
        Sparti
       </a>
       was re-founded in 1834, by a decree of King
       <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">
        Otto of Greece
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_of_Classical_Spartan_society">
        Structure of Classical Spartan society
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Constitution">
        Constitution
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Spartan_Constitution" title="Spartan Constitution">
        Spartan Constitution
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpartaGreatRhetra.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="905" data-file-width="1420" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/SpartaGreatRhetra.png/200px-SpartaGreatRhetra.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/SpartaGreatRhetra.png/300px-SpartaGreatRhetra.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/SpartaGreatRhetra.png/400px-SpartaGreatRhetra.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpartaGreatRhetra.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Structure of the Spartan Constitution
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Sparta was an
       <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">
        oligarchy
       </a>
       . The state was ruled by two
       <a href="/wiki/Diarchy" title="Diarchy">
        hereditary kings
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Kings_of_Sparta" title="List of Kings of Sparta">
        Agiad and Eurypontid
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">
        families
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       both supposedly descendants of
       <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">
        Heracles
       </a>
       and equal in authority, so that one could not act against the power and political enactments of his colleague.
      </p>
      <p>
       The duties of the kings were primarily religious, judicial, and military. They were the chief priests of the state and also maintained communication with the Delphian sanctuary, which always exercised great authority in Spartan politics. In the time of Herodotus, about 450 BC, their judicial functions had been restricted to cases dealing with heiresses, adoptions and the public roads.
       <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
        Aristotle
       </a>
       describes the kingship at Sparta as "a kind of unlimited and perpetual generalship" (Pol. iii. I285a),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while
       <a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">
        Isocrates
       </a>
       refers to the Spartans as "subject to an
       <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">
        oligarchy
       </a>
       at home, to a kingship on campaign" (iii. 24).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Civil and criminal cases were decided by a group of officials known as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ephors" title="Ephors">
        ephors
       </a>
       , as well as a council of
       <a href="/wiki/Elder_(administrative_title)" title="Elder (administrative title)">
        elders
       </a>
       known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Gerousia" title="Gerousia">
        gerousia
       </a>
       . The gerousia consisted of 28 elders over the age of 60, elected for life and usually part of the royal households, and the two kings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       High state policy decisions were discussed by this council who could then propose action alternatives to the
       <i>
        damos
       </i>
       , the collective body of Spartan citizenry, who would
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Rhetra" title="Great Rhetra">
        select one of the alternatives by voting
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The royal prerogatives were curtailed over time. Dating from the period of the Persian wars, the king lost the right to
       <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_war" title="Declaration of war">
        declare war
       </a>
       and was accompanied in the field by two ephors. He was supplanted also by the ephors in the control of foreign policy. Over time, the kings became mere figureheads except in their capacity as generals. Real power was transferred to the ephors and to the gerousia.
      </p>
      <p>
       The origins of the powers exercised by the assembly of the citizens called the
       <a href="/wiki/Apella" title="Apella">
        Apella
       </a>
       are virtually unknown because of the lack of historical documentation and Spartan state secrecy.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Citizenship">
        Citizenship
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Not all inhabitants of the Spartan state were considered to be citizens. Only those who had undertaken the Spartan education process known as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Agoge" title="Agoge">
         agoge
        </a>
       </i>
       were eligible. However, usually the only people eligible to receive the
       <i>
        agoge
       </i>
       were
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spartiates" title="Spartiates">
        Spartiates
       </a>
       , or people who could trace their ancestry to the original inhabitants of the city.
      </p>
      <p>
       There were two exceptions.
       <a href="/wiki/Trophimoi" title="Trophimoi">
        Trophimoi
       </a>
       or "foster sons" were foreign students invited to study. The Athenian general
       <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">
        Xenophon
       </a>
       , for example, sent his two sons to Sparta as trophimoi. The other exception was that the son of a helot could be enrolled as a syntrophos
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       if a Spartiate formally adopted him and paid his way. If a syntrophos did exceptionally well in training, he might be sponsored to become a Spartiate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spartans who could not afford to pay the expenses of the
       <i>
        agoge
       </i>
       could lose their citizenship.
      </p>
      <p>
       These laws meant that Sparta could not readily replace citizens lost in battle or otherwise and eventually proved near fatal to the continuance of the state as the number of citizens became greatly outnumbered by the non-citizens and, even more dangerously, the helots.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Non_citizens">
        Non citizens
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Others in the state were the
       <a href="/wiki/Perioeci" title="Perioeci">
        perioikoi
       </a>
       , who were free inhabitants of Spartan territory but were non-citizens, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Helots" title="Helots">
        helots
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-62">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the state-owned
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serfs" title="Serfs">
        serfs
       </a>
       . Descendants of non-Spartan citizens were not able to follow the
       <i>
        agoge
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Helots">
        Helots
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Helots" title="Helots">
        Helots
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Spartans were a minority of the Lakonian population. The largest class of inhabitants were the helots (in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">
        Classical Greek
       </a>
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        Εἵλωτες
       </span>
       /
       <i>
        Heílôtes
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The helots were originally free Greeks from the areas of
       <a href="/wiki/Messenia_(ancient_region)" title="Messenia (ancient region)">
        Messenia
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lakonia" title="Lakonia">
        Lakonia
       </a>
       whom the Spartans had defeated in battle and subsequently enslaved. In contrast to populations conquered by other Greek cities (e.g. the Athenian treatment of Melos), the male population was not exterminated and the women and children turned into chattel slaves. Instead, the helots were given a subordinate position in society more comparable to serfs in medieval Europe than chattel slaves in the rest of Greece.
      </p>
      <p>
       Helots did not have voting rights, although compared to non-Greek
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chattel_slaves" title="Chattel slaves">
        chattel slaves
       </a>
       in other parts of Greece they were relatively privileged. The Spartan poet
       <a href="/wiki/Tyrtaeus" title="Tyrtaeus">
        Tyrtaios
       </a>
       refers to Helots being allowed to marry and retaining 50% of the fruits of their labor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They also seem to have been allowed to practice religious rites and, according to Thucydides, own a limited amount of personal property.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some 6,000 helots accumulated enough wealth to buy their freedom, for example, in 227 BC.
      </p>
      <p>
       In other Greek city-states, free citizens were part-time soldiers who, when not at war, carried on other trades. Since Spartan men were full-time soldiers, they were not available to carry out manual labour.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The helots were used as unskilled
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serf" title="Serf">
        serfs
       </a>
       , tilling Spartan land. Helot women were often used as
       <a href="/wiki/Wet_nurse" title="Wet nurse">
        wet nurses
       </a>
       . Helots also travelled with the Spartan army as non-combatant serfs. At the last stand of the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">
        Battle of Thermopylae
       </a>
       , the Greek dead included not just the legendary three hundred Spartan soldiers but also several hundred
       <a href="/wiki/Thespiae" title="Thespiae">
        Thespian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">
        Theban
       </a>
       troops and a number of helots.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Relations between the helots and their Spartan masters were sometimes strained. There was at least one helot revolt (ca. 465–460 BC), and Thucydides remarked that "Spartan policy is always mainly governed by the necessity of taking precautions against the helots."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On the other hand, the Spartans trusted their helots enough in 479 BC to take a force of 35,000 with them to Plataea, something they could not have risked if they feared the helots would attack them or run away. Slave revolts occurred elsewhere in the Greek world, and in 413 BC 20,000 Athenian slaves ran away to join the Spartan forces occupying Attica.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       What made Sparta's relations with her slave population unique was that the helots, precisely because they enjoyed privileges such as family and property, retained their identity as a conquered people (the Messenians) and also had effective kinship groups that could be used to organize rebellion.
      </p>
      <p>
       As the Spartiate population declined and the helot population continued to grow, the imbalance of power caused increasing tension. According to Myron of Priene
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of the middle 3rd century BC:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        "They assign to the Helots every shameful task leading to disgrace. For they ordained that each one of them must wear a dogskin cap (
        <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
         κυνῆ
        </span>
        /
        <i>
         kunễ
        </i>
        ) and wrap himself in skins (
        <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
         διφθέρα
        </span>
        /
        <i>
         diphthéra
        </i>
        ) and receive a stipulated number of beatings every year regardless of any wrongdoing, so that they would never forget they were slaves. Moreover, if any exceeded the vigour proper to a slave's condition, they made death the penalty; and they allotted a punishment to those controlling them if they failed to rebuke those who were growing fat".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
         <a href="#cite_note-73">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Plutarch also states that Spartans treated the Helots "harshly and cruelly": they compelled them to drink pure wine (which was considered dangerous –
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diet_of_Ancient_Greece#Wine" title="Diet of Ancient Greece">
        wine
       </a>
       usually being cut with water) "
       <i>
        ...and to lead them in that condition into their public halls, that the children might see what a sight a drunken man is; they made them to dance low dances, and sing ridiculous songs...
       </i>
       " during
       <a href="/wiki/Syssitia" title="Syssitia">
        syssitia
       </a>
       (obligatory banquets).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Each year when the Ephors took office they ritually declared war on the helots, thereby allowing Spartans to kill them without the risk of ritual pollution.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This seems to have been done by
       <i>
        kryptai
       </i>
       (sing. κρύπτης
       <i>
        kryptēs
       </i>
       ), graduates of the
       <i>
        agoge
       </i>
       who took part in the mysterious institution known as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Crypteia" title="Crypteia">
         Krypteia
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thucydides states:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        "The helots were invited by a proclamation to pick out those of their number who claimed to have most distinguished themselves against the enemy, in order that they might receive their freedom; the object being to test them, as it was thought that the first to claim their freedom would be the most high spirited and the most apt to rebel. As many as two thousand were selected accordingly, who crowned themselves and went round the temples, rejoicing in their new freedom. The Spartans, however, soon afterwards did away with them, and no one ever knew how each of them perished."
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
         <a href="#cite_note-77">
          [73]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
         <a href="#cite_note-78">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Perioikoi">
        Perioikoi
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Perioeci" title="Perioeci">
        Perioeci
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Perioikoi came from similar origins as the helots but occupied a significantly different position in Spartan society. Although they did not enjoy full citizen-rights, they were free and not subjected to the same restrictions as the helots. The exact nature of their subjection to the Spartans is not clear, but they seem to have served partly as a kind of military reserve, partly as skilled craftsmen and partly as agents of foreign trade.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Perioikoic hoplites served increasingly with the Spartan army, explicitly at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">
        Battle of Plataea
       </a>
       , and although they may also have fulfilled functions such as the manufacture and repair of armour and weapons,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       they were increasingly integrated into the combat units of the Spartan army as the Spartiate population declined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
        Economy
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rider_BM_B1.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1680" data-file-width="1680" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Rider_BM_B1.jpg/220px-Rider_BM_B1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Rider_BM_B1.jpg/330px-Rider_BM_B1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Rider_BM_B1.jpg/440px-Rider_BM_B1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rider_BM_B1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Name_vase" title="Name vase">
          Name vase
         </a>
         of the Spartan artist known as the
         <a href="/wiki/Rider_Painter" title="Rider Painter">
          Rider Painter
         </a>
         (
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black-figure" title="Black-figure">
          black-figured
         </a>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kylix_(cup)" title="Kylix (cup)">
          kylix
         </a>
         , ca. 550–530 BC)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Spartan citizens were debarred by law from trade or manufacture, which consequently rested in the hands of the Perioikoi. The Periokoi monopoly on trade and manufacturing in one of the richest territories of Greece explains in large part the loyalty of the perioikoi to the Spartan state. Lacedaemon was rich in natural resources, fertile and blessed with a number of good natural harbors. The periokoi could exploit these resources for their own enrichment, and did.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Spartiates, on the other hand, were forbidden (in theory) from engaging in menial labor or trade, although there is evidence of Spartan sculptors,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Spartans were certainly poets, magistrates, ambassadors, and governors as well as soldiers. Allegedly, Spartans were prohibited from possessing gold and silver coins, and according to legend Spartan currency consisted of iron bars to discourage hoarding.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48LawsPower420_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-48LawsPower420-85">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was not until the 260s or 250s BC that Sparta began to mint its own coins.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The conspicuous display of wealth appears to have been discouraged, although this did not preclude the production of very fine, highly decorated bronze, ivory and wooden works of art and the production of jewellery. Archeology has produced many examples of all these objects, some of which are exquisite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Allegedly in connection with the Lycurgan Reforms (e.g. in the mid-8th Century BC), property had been divided into 9,000 equal portions as part of a massive land reform. Each citizen received one estate, a kleros, and thereafter was expected to derive his wealth from it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The land itself was worked by helots, who retained half the yield. From the other half, the Spartiate was expected to pay his mess (syssitia) fees, and the
       <i>
        agoge
       </i>
       fees for his children. However, we know nothing about whether land could be bought and sold, whether it could be inherited, if so by what system (primogeniture or equally divided among heirs), whether daughters received dowries and much more.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       What is clear is that from early on there were marked differences of wealth within the state, and these became even more serious after the law of
       <a href="/wiki/Epitadeus" title="Epitadeus">
        Epitadeus
       </a>
       , passed at some time after the
       <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">
        Peloponnesian War
       </a>
       , removed the legal prohibition of the gift or bequest of land.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By the mid-5th century, land had become concentrated in the hands of a tiny elite, and the notion of all Spartan citizens being "equals" had become a farce. By Aristotle's day (384–322 BC) citizenship had been reduced from 9,000 to less than 1,000, and then further decreased to 700 at the accession of
       <a href="/wiki/Agis_IV" title="Agis IV">
        Agis IV
       </a>
       in 244 BC. Attempts were made to remedy this situation by creating new laws. Certain penalties were imposed upon those who remained unmarried or who married too late in life. These laws, however, came too late and were ineffective in reversing the trend.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Life_in_Classical_Sparta">
        Life in Classical Sparta
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_death">
        Birth and death
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Sparta was above all a militarist state, and emphasis on military fitness began virtually at birth. Shortly after birth, a mother would bathe her child in wine to see whether the child was strong. If the child survived it was brought before the Gerousia by the child's father. The Gerousia then decided whether it was to be reared or not. It is commonly stated that if they considered it "puny and deformed", the baby was thrown into a chasm on
       <a href="/wiki/Taygetus" title="Taygetus">
        Mount Taygetos
       </a>
       known euphemistically as the
       <i>
        Apothetae
       </i>
       (Gr.,
       <i>
        ἀποθέται
       </i>
       , "Deposits").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cartledge_2001_84_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cartledge_2001_84-91">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was, in effect, a primitive form of
       <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">
        eugenics
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cartledge_2001_84_91-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cartledge_2001_84-91">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sparta is often portrayed as being unique in this matter; however, there is considerable evidence that the
       <a href="/wiki/Infanticide#Greece_and_Rome" title="Infanticide">
        killing of unwanted children
       </a>
       was practiced in other Greek regions, including Athens.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is controversy about the matter in Sparta, since excavations in the chasm only uncovered adult remains, likely belonging to criminals.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When Spartans died, marked headstones would only be granted to soldiers who died in combat during a victorious campaign or women who died either in service of a divine office or in childbirth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
        Education
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Agoge" title="Agoge">
        Agoge
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spartan_swordman.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Spartan_swordman.jpg/170px-Spartan_swordman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Spartan_swordman.jpg/255px-Spartan_swordman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Spartan_swordman.jpg/340px-Spartan_swordman.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spartan_swordman.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bronze appliqué of Spartan manufacture, possibly depicting
         <a href="/wiki/Orestes" title="Orestes">
          Orestes
         </a>
         , 550–525 BC (
         <a href="/wiki/Getty_Villa" title="Getty Villa">
          Getty Villa
         </a>
         )
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       When male Spartans began military training at age seven, they would enter the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Agoge" title="Agoge">
         agoge
        </a>
       </i>
       system. The
       <i>
        agoge
       </i>
